    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1366174483" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1366174483" />
    <a title="Adina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22189348-adina"><img alt="Adina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531917330p2/22189348.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1366174483?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adina" href="/user/show/22189348-adina">Adina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1366174483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2520211338319215967"><i>“Culture does not make people. People make culture. ”</i><br/><br/>Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is the author of Americanah and Half of a Yellow Sun, a book that highly recommend. This essay is based on a TED talk with the same title and it encourages us not to be negatively influenced by the bad reputation the word “feminism“ has built and that we need to educate our children to understand the importance of gender equality. I liked some of the arguments brought forward but I did not have the feeling she said som</span>
  <span id="freeText2520211338319215967" style="display:none"><i>“Culture does not make people. People make culture. ”</i><br /><br />Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is the author of Americanah and Half of a Yellow Sun, a book that highly recommend. This essay is based on a TED talk with the same title and it encourages us not to be negatively influenced by the bad reputation the word “feminism“ has built and that we need to educate our children to understand the importance of gender equality. I liked some of the arguments brought forward but I did not have the feeling she said something new or groundbreaking. I suspect that her TED talk was more powerful and I feel that the impact of her words is heightened in speech. <br /><br />I am a feminist. I’ve been a feminist since I was a child and one of the things that made me angry (and still does) is gender expectancy. Since we are little girls we learn that we need to be delicate, girlish, to play with dolls, to like pink (try to find girl toys that are not pink) etc. Girls who have other preferences are called boyish, rebels. I was a girly child but I hated it when my mum other people told me that a girl should/should not do this and that. The same happens when we grow older. We are expected to cook, do house chores, not to eat too much or do anything that will make us a less worthy match. We need to please men, find a husband and procreate. That is meant to be the ultimate goal in the life of a woman, even in a modern society. As Adichie also says, it is not the same thing with men, they are not expected to learn how to please a woman.<br /><br /><i>„We teach females that in relationships, compromise is what a woman is more likely to do. ”</i><br /><br />I know things are improving but there still is a long way to go. I saw some articles the other day about Amal Clooney’s speech at UN where she requested for ISIS to be held responsible for the crimes they committed. Can you guess what the titles of the story were? Amal shows her baby bump at the ONU speech!! Seriously, that was the most important thing to tell about her presence there? Don't get me wrong, it is beautiful that she has a little human growing inside her but I do not think it was the case to talk about that in those circumstances. <br /><br />That reminds me of a different problem that I face at work sometimes. During business trips I meet mainly with men and I sometimes worry what should I wear. Should I wear a dress, as I usually like to, or should I wear pants in order to be taken more seriously? Adichie touched this subject as well in her essay, discussing one lecture where she decided to dress uncomfortably and out of character in order to be respected.<br /><br /><i>"The sad truth of the matter is that when it comes to appearance, we start off with men as the standard, as the norm. Many of us think that the less feminine a woman appears, the more likely she is to be taken seriously. A man going to a business meeting doesn’t wonder about being taken seriously based on what he is wearing—but a woman does."</i><br /><br />I think people still consider that in order to be successful person (man or woman) one needs to be tough, manly and it angers me. Not everybody is like this and it should not be an aspiration. Each of us, men or women, have our wonderful qualities, we need to cherish them and be ourselves. The pressure that you can only achieve success by being "manly" also affects men who need to live to the expectations. I liked that Adichie also discusses the expectations society has for men and that they can also be harmful. <br /><br /><i>"Gender matters everywhere in the world. And I would like today to ask that we begin to dream about and plan for a different world. A fairer world. A world of happier men and happier women who are truer to themselves. And this is how to start: We must raise our daughters differently. We must also raise our sons differently."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="2520211338319215967" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1876524592" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1876524592" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1876524592?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781101911761" href="/book/show/23602569-we-should-all-be-feminists">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=essays">essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=african-lit">african-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1876524592" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12438624900606901144">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is a leading voice in African literature today. She has written three novels and one short story collection that have all won multiple awards. Two years ago she was asked by organizers of the TEDx talk to deliver a lecture on her views on feminism in the 21st century. We Should All Be Feminists is the published essay of her talk, and is a resource that is beneficial to all who read it. <br/><br/>After reading Americanah, I was curious to read one of Adichie's novels that takes pla</span>
  <span id="freeText12438624900606901144" style="display:none">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is a leading voice in African literature today. She has written three novels and one short story collection that have all won multiple awards. Two years ago she was asked by organizers of the TEDx talk to deliver a lecture on her views on feminism in the 21st century. We Should All Be Feminists is the published essay of her talk, and is a resource that is beneficial to all who read it. <br /><br />After reading Americanah, I was curious to read one of Adichie's novels that takes place fully in Africa in order to find out more about its culture. In this slim volume, Adichie expresses her views about women's place in Nigerian society. In her Igbo culture, for example, women can not make family decisions, which to her is baffling because she is the only one in her family who is interested in genealogy. In the metropolis of Lagos, women can not go to bars alone, they are viewed a certain way if they go into hotels, and single woman actually wear wedding bands to business meetings. The division of the sexes is clear, and even today women are considered a failure if they do not get married. <br /><br />Adichie stresses that human nature has not advanced in over one thousand years. Then people were valued for their physical strength so of course men were considered superior beings. Today people are valued for their wisdom and intellect and women comprise 52% of the population, yet men are still ahead. This, she stresses, is because in many cultures, men are considered the breadwinners and woman the domestic workers even if the woman has a higher level of education and a job paying more money than her male counterpart. Adichie believes that in order for women to make strides in Nigerian or any society that people have to view women from a similiar lens as men, or the divisions in society will remain rigid. <br /><br />Sharing an episode from her schooling, Adichie reminded me of an instance in my own school experience even though we grew up in different countries. In middle school her teacher told their class that the student with the highest test grade would become class monitor. She got the highest grade but a boy became class monitor. Meanwhile in my upbringing I always did better than the boys in my class in our teacher's weekly sports poll. The boys questioned why I won. Unlike Adichie, I got to keep my prize but at a cost of being teased for being better than the boys. In a society that stresses wisdom over brawniness, these two instances would be outliers rather than the norm. <br /><br />As we move further into the 21st century, Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie urges everyone to be a feminist in a positive viewpoint. Feminism does not have to mean that a person hates men, isn't happy, or does not show her girly side. Rather feminist should mean that a person strives for women to have equal access to gains in all facets of society that men have enjoyed for centuries. Only 52 short pages in length, We Should All Be Feminists can be read in under half an hour. It is a wonderful manifesto for the 21st century, and is highly recommended. 4 shining stars, downgraded simply for its short length. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12438624900606901144" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1376618341" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1376618341" />
    <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner"><img alt="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532688964p2/31461418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1376618341?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner">Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1376618341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17049046048107519672"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441740049i/16145258._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The fact that <i>feminism</i> is often considered as a negative concept is rather new to me, simply because <b>I've internalized my anger/my annoyance for <i>years</i></b> and started to point what shocked me to people only recently. <br/><br/><b>Why is that?</b> Did my family raise me in the belief that we women shouldn't speak up? Hardly. Not once did my parents implied that I shouldn't be ambitious because I was a woman. Every day of my teenage years my mother repeated to me that I should never do something - including sex - that</span>
  <span id="freeText17049046048107519672" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441740049i/16145258._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The fact that <i>feminism</i> is often considered as a negative concept is rather new to me, simply because <b>I've internalized my anger/my annoyance for <i>years</i></b> and started to point what shocked me to people only recently. <br><br><b>Why is that?</b> Did my family raise me in the belief that we women shouldn't speak up? Hardly. Not once did my parents implied that I shouldn't be ambitious because I was a woman. Every day of my teenage years my mother repeated to me that I should never do something - including sex - that I didn't want to do to fulfill other's expectations and for that, I'm grateful. I am a thirty years old woman who's successful in her work, has been in a fulfilling and loving relationship for 6 years and we don't intend to have children just quite yet. My family is okay with that and never implied that I should start a family because that's what people do.<br><br>So, why? <b>What can explain that until a few years ago I used to not stand up when faced to situations where my being was judged by gendered standards?</b> I could say that I don't know, but it wouldn't be true : I genuinely think we are the products of our society and that I had internalized so many biased statements about what I can do as a woman and what I can't that I didn't even notice them anymore. <b>This is why I find this kind of books important.</b><br><br><b>Short and highly <i>readable</i> but powerful and yes, needed</b>. Because I'll always remember the first time I expressed out loud my anger and bewilderment about a sexist situation : people (including women) told me exactly what the author has been told : <i>"ugh but you're a feminist"</i>. And yes, feminist was quite the insult here. So I started reading books. Researching. Noticing more and more generally admitted stereotypes in my everyday life. Now, when I hear someone saying things like (last week in an official meeting), <i>"children can stay here if their mum is <u><b>busy</b></u> or their father is <u><b>at work</b></u>"</i>, I frown and speak up. I'm annoying, but the fact is, I don't fucking care if I am.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>"Anger, the tone said, is particularly not good for a woman. If you are a woman, you are not supposed to express anger, because it is threatening."</b>
</blockquote>I'll add : because if you're angry people say that you're <b>"making a scene"</b>, and god forbid you answer when you're insulted! Earlier last week my little sister was publicly insulted in broad daylight because she was wearing a dress. She called me, baffled to see that <i>nobody</i> reacted and that people told her to calm down because "it was how things were" when she answered angrily in a situation where she had every right to be mad. Don't tell me it's not true that we women are supposed to be kind and pleasant : it stays, in 2015, how most people think, and you're quickly called a - sexually frustrated - bitch when you dare to say that no, thank you, I don't want to be insulted for no reasons. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>"We teach females that in relationships, compromise is what a woman is more likely to do.<br>We raise girls to see each other as competitors - not for jobs or accomplishments, which in my opinion can be a good thing, but for the attention of men."</b>
</blockquote><br>As a teacher, I can't agree more with the author's statement about how we raise children. If I'm sure that many of you are progressive in that subject, it remains that wrong ideas about what are - and more often than not in people's heads, <i>must be</i> - masculinity and feminity are spread every day and I see it in my pupils' behavior on a daily basis. <b>These stupid expectations hurt both men and women</b>.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>"What if, in raising children, we focus on <i>ability</i> instead of gender? What if we focus on <i>interest</i> instead of gender?"</b>
</blockquote><br><b>That's why I urge you to read this short essay</b> and I'm going to <s>shove this book in my friends' throat</s> gently and nicely advice my friends to read it. If it can make people more interested in these issues, it would already be a positive step. <br><br><i>For more of my reviews, please visit:</i><br><blockquote>
  <a href="https://meetthebookworld.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487851828i/22057164._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="17049046048107519672" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Samadrita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13266158-samadrita"><img alt="Samadrita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461258628p2/13266158.jpg" /></a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1016300890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17204618162328186947">This is the published version of CNA's famous Tedx talk which I had put on my youtube <i>'watch later'</i> list and never quite managed to get to in the end.<br/><br/>It's so perfectly presented and written (albeit in a very simplistic manner with little to no token humor thrown in to engage a live audience) that I don't know how to review this except by saying I nodded my head vigorously to every logical inference Adichie drew from her own experiences and those of her family and acquaintances in Nigeria and the</span>
  <span id="freeText17204618162328186947" style="display:none">This is the published version of CNA's famous Tedx talk which I had put on my youtube <i>'watch later'</i> list and never quite managed to get to in the end.<br /><br />It's so perfectly presented and written (albeit in a very simplistic manner with little to no token humor thrown in to engage a live audience) that I don't know how to review this except by saying I nodded my head vigorously to every logical inference Adichie drew from her own experiences and those of her family and acquaintances in Nigeria and the U.S. Even her anecdote about a friend named Louis who kept telling her, <i>"I don't see what you mean by things being different and harder for women. Maybe it was so in the past but not now. Everything is fine now for women."</i> rung true in my ears because I have a friend exactly like Louis who used to think everything is hunky dory for women these days until I successfully talked him out of that delusion. My only grouse is that I would have loved for CNA to expand on the core themes of her lecture and write a series of full-fledged essays on the topics instead of publishing this short talk. <br /><br />Also here's what she says about those who raise objections to the term <i>'feminism'</i> - <br /><blockquote>
  <i>Some people ask: "Why the word feminist? Why not just say you are a believer in human rights, or something like that?" Because that would be dishonest. Feminism is, of course, part of human rights in general - but to choose to use the vague expression human rights is to deny the specific and particular problem of gender. It would be a way of pretending it was not women who have, for centuries, been excluded. It would be a way of denying that the problem of gender targets women. That the problem was not about being human, but specifically about being a female human.</i>
</blockquote><br />There. Thank you for saying that Chimamanda. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17204618162328186947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14862601255957291591">Like so, so many others, I saw the TED talk as it swept through popular culture (later with the help of Beyoncé), and for that reason I didn't mark this volume very high on my to-be-read pile.   <span id="freeText14862601255957291591" style="display:none">Like so, so many others, I saw the TED talk as it swept through popular culture (later with the help of Beyoncé), and for that reason I didn't mark this volume very high on my to-be-read pile. However, after receiving it as a just-because gift, I found it served me well one morning while waiting for the train.<br /><br />I was happy to read that, though only slightly, she had expanded on some ideas for the print version of her talk. This is an absolutely fine introduction to feminism. She articulates important ideas. However, it is not particularly groundbreaking, and I would have loved for this volume to have been expanded more to allow for a deeper exploration of the ideas she presents. At any rate, I am delighted with how passionately this book, the lecture and its ideas, have been taken up.</span>

<div id="review_1273620016" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1273620016" />
    <a title="Erin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32799998-erin"><img alt="Erin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469767858p2/32799998.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1273620016?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin" href="/user/show/32799998-erin">Erin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32799998-erin?shelf=popsugar-challenge-2018">popsugar-challenge-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32799998-erin?shelf=march-2018">march-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32799998-erin?shelf=2018-around-the-year-in-52-books">2018-around-the-year-in-52-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32799998-erin?shelf=written-by-women">written-by-women</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1273620016" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12731717915186237122">FEMINIST: A person who believes in the social, political, and economic equality of the sexes.<br/><br/>I was raised by two feminist my mother and my father. Though my father would never call himself a feminist not because its a dirty word but because he believes as does Ms. Adiche that we should all be feminist. My mother was a feminist but she too would never call herself that, because she was told on multiple occasions by other feminist that she wasn't one because she chose to stay home and raise my sis</span>
  <span id="freeText12731717915186237122" style="display:none">FEMINIST: A person who believes in the social, political, and economic equality of the sexes.<br /><br />I was raised by two feminist my mother and my father. Though my father would never call himself a feminist not because its a dirty word but because he believes as does Ms. Adiche that we should all be feminist. My mother was a feminist but she too would never call herself that, because she was told on multiple occasions by other feminist that she wasn't one because she chose to stay home and raise my sister and me. These women liked to tell my mother that she was setting women back 20 years and that she was being controlled by my father. <br /><br />None of you guys ever got the pleasure of meeting my mother but I can tell you one thing..<br /><br />No One Controlled Donna Patton EVER! <br /><br />I feel as though both feminist and non feminist fall in to the trap of thinking that you can't be a stay at home mom and a strong independent feminist at the same time. My dad didn't force my mother to quit being a nurse and stay home. My mother made that decision, because she controlled her own life. My dad may have made the money but my mom made all the decisions. My dad is strong enough to handle a strong woman. <br /><br />In We Should All Be Feminist Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie simply asks that we take a look at how society effects gender dynamics. Society tells us you can't be both feminine and be taken seriously. Society tells us that if you hadn't been wearing that miniskirt you wouldn't have been raped. Society tells us that men can't be sensitive they must always be hard and strong. <br /><br />Ms. Adiche asks us to look at all the little ways societal gender roles are ingrained in all of us. I still expect the guy to pay when we go out to eat. Does that make me less of a feminist? I don't think so but it probably does to those women who looked down on my mother for staying home to raise her kids. <br /><br />A must read for All genders.<br /><br />Popsugar 2018: A book about feminism<br />Around the Year in 52 Books: An Own Voices Book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12731717915186237122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10676685148738800750">I wish this were required reading for everyone. I have been recommending it to every person I know. It’s a short book, took me around an hour and it is so worth it. Based on a TED Talk by the same name, Adichie discusses the weight and stereotypes around the word “feminist” and why we should all identify as such. I recommend this book as a gateway to gender studies and feminism because it is very accessible, especially to those with no experience in reading/studying these subjects. My mother is </span>
  <span id="freeText10676685148738800750" style="display:none">I wish this were required reading for everyone. I have been recommending it to every person I know. It’s a short book, took me around an hour and it is so worth it. Based on a TED Talk by the same name, Adichie discusses the weight and stereotypes around the word “feminist” and why we should all identify as such. I recommend this book as a gateway to gender studies and feminism because it is very accessible, especially to those with no experience in reading/studying these subjects. My mother is not a reader but she picked this up on my suggestion and loved it.<br /><br />— Patricia Elzie<br /><br /><br />from The Best Books We Read In January 2017: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2017/02/01/riot-round-best-books-read-january-2017/">http://bookriot.com/2017/02/01/riot-r...</a> <br />____________________<br /><br /><br />This is a SHORT book. Almost more an essay, it is based on Adichie’s TED talk of the same name. Other than wanting SO MUCH MORE, it was essentially perfect. I read an ebook but have every intention of collecting paperbacks to scatter everywhere that I go.<br /><br />— Annika Barranti Klein<br /><br /><br />from The Best Books We Read In December 2016: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2017/01/03/riot-round-best-books-read-december-3/">http://bookriot.com/2017/01/03/riot-r...</a></span>

<div id="review_1575816586" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1575816586" />
    <a title="Fabian {Councillor}" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40792319-fabian-councillor"><img alt="Fabian {Councillor}" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599133151p2/40792319.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1575816586?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian {Councillor}" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian {Councillor}" href="/user/show/40792319-fabian-councillor">Fabian {Councillor}</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Fabian {Councillor} by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/23304719-anne">Anne </a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40792319-fabian-councillor?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40792319-fabian-councillor?shelf=essays">essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40792319-fabian-councillor?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40792319-fabian-councillor?shelf=chimamanda-ngozi-adichie">chimamanda-ngozi-adichie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40792319-fabian-councillor?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1575816586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7238752002858561942"><b>If you have only thirty minutes of time left, then listen to the author, Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie, reading her essay <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hg3umXU_qWc' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a> for free.</b> Whether you are a feminist or not, whether you are male or female, you won't regret listening to this, regardless of what your current view on the much-debated topic of feminism is like. Either she will open your eyes to some aspects you never thought about before, or she will convince you of your already established opinion. However it turns out for you, in my opin</span>
  <span id="freeText7238752002858561942" style="display:none"><b>If you have only thirty minutes of time left, then listen to the author, Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie, reading her essay <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hg3umXU_qWc" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> for free.</b> Whether you are a feminist or not, whether you are male or female, you won't regret listening to this, regardless of what your current view on the much-debated topic of feminism is like. Either she will open your eyes to some aspects you never thought about before, or she will convince you of your already established opinion. However it turns out for you, in my opinion, Chimamanda is an amazing person who deserves all the attention for what she put into words with her nonfictional essay on feminism.<br /><br />In our modern culture, the word feminism is always associated with a negative concept, and I don't blame anyone for this - not as long as some German politicians want to equalize the female amount of <i>little traffic light men</i> because they feel like gender equality should be visible at traffic lights. Shouldn't it be more important to focus on equal opportunities at work, on the (sadly) still existing way women are looked at as something less in a lot of countries? Apparently not, if you look at what some politicians say nowadays.<br /><br />In her essay, Chimamanda talks about how, especially in Nigeria, but also in a lot of other countries, young men are taught how they need to be "hard men", how they need to be physically strong and take over a leading role in order to take care of the "weaker" women. And she criticizes exactly this - with good reason. <br /><br /><blockquote>Recently a young woman was gang raped in a university in Nigeria. And the response of many young Nigerians, both male and female, was something along the lines of this: "Yes, rape is wrong. But what is a girl doing in a room with four boys?" Now if we can forget the horrible inhumanity of that response, these Nigerians have been raised to think of women as inherently guilty, and have been raised to expect so little of men that the idea of men as savage beings without any control is somehow acceptable. We teach girls shame. <i>Close your legs.</i> <i>Cover yourself.</i> We make them feel as though by being born female they're already guilty of something.</blockquote><br />I can only talk about my <b>own experience</b> now as a young man about how it is in Germany, but what I had to learn so far was this: A lot of people have no idea of what the concept of feminism truly means, they think of it as something some weirdly-brained writers of bad self-help books must have invented in order to torture humans with their implausible beliefs. And this is something that angers me, because not only do some men find pleasure in treating women like shit, some women also encourage them to do so. But inspite of that, <b>this is no excuse for rape</b>. Nothing can excuse something as horrible as rape, something that should never be wished upon any person. Never ever.<br /><br />I am angry about this. I am angry about the fact that if you refer to "Studenten" (German for "students") instead of "Studentinnen und Studenten" (German for "female and male students"), you will immediately be verbally attacked because you downgraded women, but if you treat women like garbage, you will be praised as the ultimate male hero everyone else has to look up to. I am angry that in order to be looked at as a "man", you have to raise yourself up from women - otherwise there will always be people who start laughing when you call yourself a man and who are going to say that someone like you could only be a "kiddie". Or at least that's what society tends to make us believe.<br /><br />Chimamanda also mentioned that a lot of people argue how female apes bow down to male apes, and after all, apes are our relatives, right? Her answer to this, simple and humorous alike: "But the point is, we're not apes. Apes also live on trees and have earth worms for breakfast, but we don't." And she couldn't be more correct about this. After all, men are human just the way women are human, except for the obvious biological differences, and if you put this into a simple mathematical equation, it would be something along the lines of this:<br /><b>men = human<br />women = human</b><br />Thus, the following must apply as well:<b> men = women</b><br />(See? Sometimes maths is good for something after all!)<br /><br /><br /><b>More people need to read this book. More women, but also more men, because the term "feminist" doesn't mean that you need to be a woman in order to be a feminist.</b> It is sad to see how only a handful of male Goodreads users have read this book, even though Chimamanda doesn't only talk about how women need to fight for their rights, she also talks about how men need to learn to accept women as equal.<br /><br />This essay should <b>definitely</b> be required reading in school. You can't learn the lessons Chimamanda teaches in her text early enough. Even more importantly, she doesn't say that you <i>need</i> to be a <i>feminist</i> (even though the title implies so). No, she talks to you about the situation in Nigeria and other countries, and she makes you think about it and allows you to form your own opinion. And that's what I truly respect her for.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>You are a feminist? Fine, then you might still learn something from this essay.<br />You are no feminist? Good, that's even more of a reason for you to click on that link on top of my review.</u>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="7238752002858561942" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1914358556" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1914358556" />
    <a title="Julie " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5210022-julie"><img alt="Julie " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356906542p2/5210022.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1914358556?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie " href="/user/show/5210022-julie">Julie </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=paperback">paperback</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=quarterly-literary-fiction-box">quarterly-literary-fiction-box</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1914358556" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1254013437544613511">We Should All be Feminists by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is a 2014 Random House publication. I was provided a copy of this book by Quarterly Literary fiction box. (<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://quarterly.co/products/literar'>https://quarterly.co/products/literar</a>...)<br/><br/><br/>A thousand times I have intended to get a copy of this essay, but always got distracted before following through. <br/><br/>Recently, I discovered this book was both influential and inspirational to Britt Bennett, author of ‘The Mothers.’ So, with her stamp of approval added to the overwhelmingly favorable</span>
  <span id="freeText1254013437544613511" style="display:none">We Should All be Feminists by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is a 2014 Random House publication. I was provided a copy of this book by Quarterly Literary fiction box. (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://quarterly.co/products/literar">https://quarterly.co/products/literar</a>...)<br /><br /><br />A thousand times I have intended to get a copy of this essay, but always got distracted before following through. <br /><br />Recently, I discovered this book was both influential and inspirational to Britt Bennett, author of ‘The Mothers.’ So, with her stamp of approval added to the overwhelmingly favorable reviews posted, I finally sat down and read through this award winning essay. <br /><br /><br />THIS!! <br /><br />This published essay is adapted from the author’s TED talk by the same name, which I haven’t seen. But the work is obviously written from the heart and although the author and I are from entirely different worlds, our thought process is exactly the same.<br /><br /> So, often I have lamented the lack of instruction our young men have in regards to proper behavior and treatment of women. Boys will be boys, is still so common and accepted, and yes, we must do better!! <br /> <br />I loved the message here, which is inspirational, but also eloquently pleads the case for feminism, dispelling that common stereotype the word conjures up for many people. <br /><br />I assure you, I am not loud, shrill, or preachy, and definitely do not hate men. But, I have had to fight the same battles for equality and fair treatment over the years and can relate to the author’s arguments and personal experiences. <br /><br />“If we do something over and over again, it becomes normal. If only boys are made class monitor, then at some point we will all think, at least unconsciously, that the class monitor has to be a boy.”<br /><br />This is true, and can be applied to many situations in life, requiring diligence on our part to avoid becoming complacent or accepting of certain behaviors. <br /><br />I could go on and on about the wisdom and insight of this essay, but I want you to read it for yourself and pass it along, not only to your daughters, but to your sons as well. <br /><br /> 5 stars<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1254013437544613511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jnahL5n9GSL75+YCaNvyxbYPEbvDTZ8SjfWm08mV3SAOC/2KvQ+2CY1V0HAe35a26lPpEblVBIiNnX3/OwcWeg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gTw7l//YVciG+75/NgS1Lg1R3+FIhqgOMNm4eaXjYIIBQWcy2yr64/BJiA1AANFdUQ0nSzKeM5QwsWNVV3Gr2A==')})

The portrait that emerges is not only one of an incredibly insightful woman continually growing to understand herself and our society, but also one of our culture.\n\nBad Feminist is a sharp, funny, <script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_25489625'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25489625-between-the-world-and-me?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Between the World and Me<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/1214964.Ta_Nehisi_Coates?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Ta-Nehisi Coates<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.39 avg rating &mdash; 241,000 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2015\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer184295466972018721\">“This is your country, this is your world, this is your body, and you must find some way to live within the all of it.” In a profound work that pivots from the biggest questions about American history and ideals to the most intimate concerns of a fat<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText184295466972018721\" style=\"display:none\">“This is your country, this is your world, this is your body, and you must find some way to live within the all of it.” In a profound work that pivots from the biggest questions about American history and ideals to the most intimate concerns of a father for his son, Ta-Nehisi Coates offers a powerful new framework for understanding our nation’s history and current crisis. Americans have built an empire on the idea of “race,” a falsehood that damages us all but falls most heavily on the bodies of black women and men—bodies exploited through slavery and segregation, and, today, threatened, locked up, and murdered out of all proportion. What is it like to inhabit a black body and find a way to live within it? And how can we all honestly reckon with this fraught history and free ourselves from its burden?  Between the World and Me is Ta-Nehisi Coates’s attempt to answer these questions in a letter to his adolescent son. Coates shares with his son—and readers—the story of his awakening to the truth about his place in the world through a series of revelatory experiences, from Howard University to Civil War battlefields, from the South Side of Chicago to Paris, from his childhood home to the living rooms of mothers whose children’s lives were taken as American plunder. Beautifully woven from personal narrative, reimagined history, and fresh, emotionally charged reportage, Between the World and Me clearly illuminates the past, bracingly confronts our present, and offers a transcendent vision for a way forward.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"184295466972018721\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div 65,412 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2018\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer18373893656079899762\">In this breakout book, Ijeoma Oluo explores the complex reality of today\'s racial landscape--from white privilege and police brutality to systemic discrimination and the Black Lives Matter movement--offering straightforward clarity that readers need <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText18373893656079899762\" style=\"display:none\">In this breakout book, Ijeoma Oluo explores the complex reality of today\'s racial landscape--from white privilege and police brutality to systemic discrimination and the Black Lives Matter movement--offering straightforward clarity that readers need to contribute to the dismantling of the racial divideIn So You Want to Talk About Race, Editor at Large of The Establishment Ijeoma Oluo offers a contemporary, accessible take on the racial landscape in America, addressing head-on such issues as privilege, police brutality, intersectionality, micro-aggressions, the Black Lives Matter movement, and the &quot;N&quot; word. Perfectly positioned to bridge the gap between people of color and white Americans struggling with race complexities, Oluo answers the questions readers don\'t dare ask, and explains the concepts that continue to elude everyday Americans. Oluo is an exceptional writer with a rare ability to be straightforward, funny, and effective in her coverage of sensitive, hyper-charged issues in America. Her messages are passionate but finely tuned, and crystalize ideas that would otherwise be vague by empowering them with aha-moment clarity.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie">About Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1474446019p3/11291.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
28,938
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1171423323338573435">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie grew up in Nigeria.<br/><br/>Her work has been translated into over thirty languages and has appeared in various publications, including <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The O. Henry Prize Stories</i>, the <i>Financial Times</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. She is the author of the novels <i>Purple Hibiscus</i>, which won the Commonwealth Writers’ Prize and the Hurston/Wright Legacy Award; <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i>, which won t</span>
  <span id="freeText1171423323338573435" style="display:none">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie grew up in Nigeria.<br /><br />Her work has been translated into over thirty languages and has appeared in various publications, including <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The O. Henry Prize Stories</i>, the <i>Financial Times</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. She is the author of the novels <i>Purple Hibiscus</i>, which won the Commonwealth Writers’ Prize and the Hurston/Wright Legacy Award; <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i>, which won the Orange Prize and was a National Book Critics Circle Award Finalist and a <i>New York Times</i> Notable Book; and <i>Americanah</i>, which won the National Book Critics Circle Award and was named one of <i>The New York Times</i> Top Ten Best Books of 2013. Ms. Adichie is also the author of the story collection <i>The Thing Around Your Neck</i>.<br /><br />Ms. Adichie has been invited to speak around the world. Her 2009 TED Talk, <i>The Danger of A Single Story</i>, is now one of the most-viewed TED Talks of all time. Her 2012 talk <i>We Should All Be Feminists</i> has a started a worldwide conversation about feminism, and was published as a book in 2014.<br /><br />Her most recent book, <i>Dear Ijeawele, or a Feminist Manifesto in Fifteen Suggestions</i>, was published in March 2017.<br /><br />A recipient of a MacArthur Foundation Fellowship, Ms. Adichie divides her time between the United States and Nigeria.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1171423323338573435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/42278179-we-should-all-be-feminists">Trivia About We Should All Be ...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
        <div class="mediumText">No trivia or quizzes yet. <a href="/trivia/work/42278179-we-should-all-be-feminists">Add some now &raquo;</a></div>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>


      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/42278179-we-should-all-be-feminists">Quotes from We Should All Be ...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;We teach girls to shrink themselves, to make themselves smaller. We say to girls, you can have ambition, but not too much. You should aim to be successful, but not too successful. Otherwise, you would threaten the man. Because I am female, I am expected to aspire to marriage. I am expected to make my life choices always keeping in mind that marriage is the most important. Now marriage can be a source of joy and love and mutual support but why do we teach girls to aspire to marriage and we don’t teach boys the same? We raise girls to see each other as competitors not for jobs or accomplishments, which I think can be a good thing, but for the attention of men. We teach girls that they cannot be sexual beings in the way that boys are.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/990500-we-teach-girls-to-shrink-themselves-to-make-themselves-smaller">1940 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Some people ask: “Why the word feminist? Why not just say you are a believer in human rights, or something like that?” Because that would be dishonest. Feminism is, of course, part of human rights in general—but to choose to use the vague expression human rights is to deny the specific and particular problem of gender. It would be a way of pretending that it was not women who have, for centuries, been excluded. It would be a way of denying that the problem of gender targets women.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1320706-some-people-ask-why-the-word-feminist-why-not-just">790 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/42278179-we-should-all-be-feminists">More quotes&hellip;</a>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
